                            <div class="player-info-box-header team-dodgers"><div class="player-info-box-name"><h1>Yasmani Grandal</h1><div class="player-info-box-name-team"><a href="/teams/dodgers">Los Angeles Dodgers</a></div></div><div class="player-info-box-item">Age: 29</div><div class="player-info-box-item">Bats/Throws: B/R</div>

            <div class="catchall300" style='width:300px; overflow:hidden;float:right;'><!-- RoS_300x250 --> <div id ='div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13' style='width:300px; height:250px; overflow:hidden;'><script type='text/javascript'>if ($('#div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13').is(':visible')) {googletag.cmd.push(function () { googletag.display('div-gpt-ad-1349421924281-13'); });}</script></div></div>

        <tr id="so5" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>The switch-hitting Grandal was a highly-touted prospect in the Reds system but was deemed somewhat expendable given the fact that they also had the equally, if not more, impressive Devin Mesoraco knocking on the major league door as well. So he was shipped to the Padres as part of of a big prospect trade during the offseason where he was expected to quickly ascend to the major league roster. While with the Padres Triple-A affiliate, Grandal posted a solid .186 isolated slugging percentage and actually walked more than he struck out. With a struggling Nick Hundley landing on the disabled list and only journeyman John Baker behind the dish, Grandal's promotion was a no-brainer. At the big league level he posted numbers very similar to those at Triple-A, but had his season interrupted with an oblique strain. He returned shortly thereafter and finished the season, batting an impressive .325 for the month of September. Grandal was slated to open the season as the Padres primary backstop, but a 50-game suspension for testing positive for elevated levels of testosterone now pushes his 2013 debut to early June. His numbers were never considered gaudy at any point in time, so it is not yet known how this will affect his overall performance. However, missing the first two months of the season will certainly put a crimp on his overall fantasy value. (Howard Bender)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Grandal was slated to open the 2013 season as the Padres primary backstop but a 50-game suspension for testing positive for elevated levels of testosterone will keep him out for the first two months of the season. His batting eye and overall plate discipline aren't likely to change, but you might want to hedge your bets on his power production a little bit until we see how he performs upon his return.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so6" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Fantasy baseballers have been waiting for the Yasmani Grandal hype machine to pay its dividends for some time now. Last season, owners patiently waited for the young catcher to return from his 50-game PED suspension, just to see his season prematurely ended thanks to a torn ACL. In the past three seasons, Grandal is third among catchers with a minimum of 250 PAs with a .380 on-base percentage. We should see more of the same on-base guy with a successful return from rehab -- and he makes enough contact (17.1% career strikeout rate) and has enough power (.157 career isolated slugging percentage) to matter in the batting average and home run categories, too. Grandal is <a href="http://www.utsandiego.com/news/2013/dec/21/padres-mlb-benoit-smith/" style="color: #000000; text-decoration: none;">expected</a> to start the 2014 season on the disabled list, but should be considered a late-round stash in mixers. Even with all the question marks, he's a top talent with pedigree and good peripherals. (Alan Harrison)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Adding insult (PED suspension) to injury (ACL surgery) -- or the other way around -- Yasmani Grandal did not have a great year in 2013. This season still bring the promise of patience, contact, and power. That's a stellar mix, especially from a catcher, and that's why he's still very relevant despite the question marks.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so7" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>For a guy with a career .245 batting average, Grandal is plenty capable of putting up a year hitting .270 with an above average on-base percentage and upper-teens homer totals. Though noticeably weaker from the right side of the plate, he boasts a solid line-drive stroke from the southern end. It would be foolish to expect a huge increase in power because of his swing qualities, but do look for a lot of doubles and average home run power. With the PED and injury history, there is some risk to betting on Grandal, but his upside and pedigree more than make up for these concerns. Expect a reliable up-the-middle player who won't hurt you on either side of the game. (Dan Farnsworth)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Don't let Grandal's numbers the last two years fool you; he is poised to put up a solid offensive year with a better supporting cast than he has enjoyed in his young career.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so8" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Yasmani Grandal’s development from top catching prospect to reliable fantasy backstop has been stunted over the years due to injury and suspension, though he finally broke out in 2015 — sort of. On the surface, his overall offensive production was essentially a mirror image of his 2014 season, when he posted slightly above-average offensive production. But it’s key to note that Grandal, 27, was bothered by left shoulder inflammation in the second half last summer, causing his on-base plus slugging percentage to plunge nearly 430 points after the all-star break. (In fact, his 159 weighted runs created-plus led all catchers with at least 200 plate appearances in the first half.) When he’s on, Grandal is a legitimate power threat: his 16.3% home run rate on fly balls was backed by a 299-foot average batted ball distance that was tops among all qualified catchers for 2015, and he’s now posted back-to-back seasons with at least 15 dingers. He was also able to get surgery on his troubled left shoulder immediately after the postseason, so presumably he’ll be 100% for spring training. Investing heavily in catchers is always risky, but Grandal’s underwhelming .234/.353/.403 slash line last year could be enough to scare off owners who forgot about his all-star, first-half form. Expect Grandal to be slightly discounted this March but an attractive target for 20-home-run upside behind the plate. (<a href="https://twitter.com/Karl_de_Vries">Karl de Vries</a>)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Yasmani Grandal’s 2015 season was divided between two halves: before and after he experienced shoulder problems. Now that he’s had surgery and a full winter to rest, however, owners should eye him as a rare catcher who has the ability to deliver 20 home runs.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
        <tr id="so9" style="background-color: #FFF; display:none;"><td colspan="5" padding:3px; text-align:justify;"><div class="m_stories" style="font-size:10pt"><center><table><tr><td style="width:615px; padding-right:7px; text-align:justify;" valign="top"><b>Profile: </b>Death. Taxes. Yasmani Grandal will never win a batting title. The Yasmanian Devil put up a monster home run pace that would've ranked among the game's best had he not rested every fourth day. He always had power, but he really cranked it up a notch in 2016. Given the league-wide power surge, it's hard to make much of a home run-to-fly ball rate that clocked in more than 10 percentage points better than his career mark. Improvements to his batted ball peripherals validate his gains, but this author is reluctant to buy fully into them. Regardless, Grandal projects to be one of the game's premier power threats behind the dish. He's cut from the same cloth as Russell Martin and Brian McCann — all power, no average — but playing-time concerns render him slightly less valuable than his  contemporaries. (In OBP leagues, as part of one of baseball's best offenses, he could be the best of the three despite fewer plate appearances.) The trio's average draft positions (ADPs) are all similar, so you'll either get Grandal at a discount (if you think the power is real) or at slightly above cost (if you are wrong). Regardless, his price isn't outrageous, and he has established a nice floor for himself the past three years, making it hard to consider him a bad buy. He's a top-10 catcher. (Alex Chamberlain)<br /><br /><b>The Quick Opinion: </b>Grandal's typical 25-homer power suddenly became 35-homer power in 2016. His peripherals support the bump, but it coincided with the league's power spike so it's hard to know if it was legitimate or sustainable. Regardless of your take, his floor plus upside makes him an easy top-10 pick behind the dish.</td></tr></table></center></div></td></tr>
    </tr>
</table>
